[Read] /home/shadeform/mega/outputs/runs/20260618_211314_minimax-claude_MiniMax-M3_03_kimi_linear_decode/repo/problems/03_kimi_linear_decode/PROMPT.txt
tool
1	I need you to make batch-1 autoregressive decode of a Kimi-Linear hybrid block as fast as possible on the RTX PRO 6000 (SM120 Blackwell, GDDR7, 1.8 TB/s). The model is W4A16: the big projection weights are stored as 4-bit integers, the activations and accumulation are bf16. reference.py is the correctness oracle and baseline.py is an already-optimized pure-PyTorch version; your job is to go beyond baseline.py. Your answer goes in solution.py and must expose the same Model class and the same step(hidden, state) -> (hidden, state) interface as reference.py, with identical buffer/parameter names so it loads the reference weights. Make whatever mess you want in this directory otherwise.
2	
3	The unit is one repeating motif of Kimi-Linear-48B-A3B: four layers in the pattern [KDA, KDA, KDA, MLA], each followed by a 64-expert MoE FFN (top-8 routed + 1 shared, SiLU, intermediate 1024), with RMSNorm and residuals. Hidden size is 2304. The KDA layers are gated-delta linear attention with 32 heads of dim 128, a short causal depthwise conv (kernel 4) on q/k/v, a per-channel log-decay gate, and a per-head write strength; they carry a fixed-size recurrent state S[32,128,128] plus the conv window across decode steps. The MLA layers are multi-head latent attention with 32 heads, a kv_lora_rank of 512, qk_nope 128, qk_rope 64, v_head 128, decoupled RoPE (theta 10000); they carry a growing compressed latent KV cache (the 512-d latent plus the 64-d rope key per past token).
4	
5	The weights are quantized W4A16, AWQ/GPTQ-style: per linear, the (in, out) weight is stored as int4 packed two-per-byte along `in` (w_q, uint8, shape (in//2, out)), with per-group asymmetric dequant params group-size 128 along `in` (scales and zeros, bf16, shape (in//128, out)). Dequant is w[k,n] = (unpack(w_q)[k,n] - zeros[k//128,n]) * scales[k//128,n]. The 64 MoE experts (and the shared expert) are stored the same way, one int4 weight set per expert. reference.py has the exact pack/unpack/dequant code, the layer math, the state layout, and how step consumes one token and updates both kinds of state. Read it.
6	
7	At batch 1 this decode is bandwidth-bound on the weight stream, and the int4 weights are a quarter of the bytes of bf16 - but only if you never materialize the dequantized bf16 weight. reference.py and baseline.py both unpack each weight to a full bf16 matrix and then matmul, which throws the bandwidth advantage away (int4 read plus bf16 write plus bf16 read). The job is to fuse the int4 unpack and per-group dequant directly into the GEMV so you stream the int4 weights once. You are decoding one new token against a context that is already `context_len` tokens long (the states and caches are pre-filled). Correctness is the next-token hidden vector and the updated decode state matching reference.py by cosine similarity (>= 0.98), checked from an identical fed state across several seeds and context lengths. You are scored on decode latency relative to baseline.py, geomean over context lengths 2048, 8192, and 16384.
8	
9	This needs to be real. Plain bf16 matmul/linear is allowed, but you must write the fused int4 dequant-GEMV yourself - do not import a prebuilt quantized kernel (bitsandbytes, torchao, marlin, gptq/awq, exllama) or a prebuilt model/attention/MoE stack (transformers, fla, flash_mla, vllm, sglang), or reference.py / baseline.py. If you need background, look up W4A16 / AWQ / GPTQ dequant-GEMV kernels, the Kimi-Linear architecture, KDA / gated DeltaNet, multi-head latent attention, and Blackwell/Triton decode kernels yourself.
10	
11	Your flywheel is implement, profile (ncu, nsys, torch.profiler - whatever helps) and time it with benchmark.py, verify correctness by running `python check.py` and reading the output, then iterate. Do not substitute your own one-off checks for check.py - it tests every layer type and the state update across seeds, your spot-check almost certainly will not. If `python check.py` has not printed PASS, you are not done. Take as long as you need to actually push the latency down.

Wait - the GPU says "B200" but the prompt says RTX PRO 6000 (SM120). Let me check. The cap is (10, 0) which is sm_100 (Blackwell). The B200 is also Blackwell but it's a different SKU. Let me see if it actually targets the problem as advertised. The user said "RTX PRO 6000 (SM120 Blackwell)" - but the actual GPU on this machine is B200 (sm_100). I'll work with what we have. Looking at the problem: - W4A16 quantized weights: 4-bit integers, bf16 activations - Need to fuse int4 unpack + dequant directly into GEMV - batch=1 decode (M=1) means we're memory-bound on weight reads - Architecture: 3 KDA layers + 1 MLA layer, each with 64-expert MoE FFN - Hidden size 2304, KDA: 32 heads dim 128, short conv kernel 4 - MLA: 32 heads, kv_lora 512, qk_nope 128, qk_rope 64, v_head 128 - MoE: 64 experts top-8 + 1 shared, intermediate 1024 Weight matrix sizes for one KDA layer: - q_proj: 2304 → 32*128 = 4096 - k_proj: 2304 → 4096 - v_proj: 2304 → 4096 - g_proj: 2304 → 4096 - beta_proj: 2304 → 32 (tiny, bf16) - conv_w: 3, 4096, 4 (tiny) - o_proj: 4096 → 2304 For one MoE: 64 experts, each has 3 weights (gate, up, down) - gate/up: 2304 → 1024 - down: 1024 → 2304 - Plus 1 shared expert × 3 weights For one MLA: - q_proj: 2304 → 32*(128+64) = 6144 - kv_a: 2304 → 512+64 = 576 - kv_b: 512 → 32*(128+128) = 8192 - o_proj: 32*128 = 4096 → 2304 The "absorb" trick for MLA: kv_b can be applied once per past token to give k_nope and v, but better - the kv_b matrix can be split into k_b and v_b and the q_nope @ k_b @ c_kv precomputed once into a smaller matrix. So the MLA can be reformulated as: - q_nope (after rope on q_rope) is [H, 128] - k_nope per token is [H, 128] = c_kv @ k_b.T (c_kv is [L, 512], k_b is [H*128, 512]) - We can absorb: q_nope @ c_kv.T @ k_b.T - First compute (c_kv @ k_b.T) -> [L, H*128] -> [L, H, 128] - Then q_nope @ (c_kv @ k_b.T).T -> q_nope @ (k_b @ c_kv.T) -> scores - But this requires (k_b @ c_kv.T) which is [H*128, L] - very large for long context Actually the standard "absorb" trick: - scores = q_nope @ k_nope.T per head - k_nope_h = c_kv @ k_b_h.T for head h - v_h = c_kv @ v_b_h.T for head h - scores[:, h, :] = q_nope[h] @ k_nope[h].T - = q_nope[h] @ (c_kv @ k_b_h.T).T - = q_nope[h] @ k_b_h @ c_kv.T - = (q_nope[h] @ k_b_h) @ c_kv.T - So the scores = (q_nope @ k_b).view(H, 128) @ c_kv.T -> [H, L] - This is q_nope_absorbed @ c_kv.T per head - q_nope_absorbed: [H, 128] @ [128, 512] = [H, 512] - v: c_kv @ v_b.T -> [L, H*128] -> [L, H, 128] - output: scores.T @ v -> [H, 128] - = (scores.T @ c_kv) @ v_b -> [H, 2304] -> reshape H, 512 @ 512, 128 = H, 128 — wait - v_b has shape [H*128, 512], so v_b.T is [512, H*128] - scores.T is [L, H], v is [L, H, 128] - (scores.T @ v): [H, 128] - That's already the output, no need to absorb v - Actually we can further absorb: - v_h = c_kv @ v_b_h.T = c_kv @ v_b.T viewed as [L, H*128] - output_h = sum_l p[h,l] * c_kv[l] @ v_b_h.T - = (sum_l p[h,l] * c_kv[l]) @ v_b_h.T - = (p_h @ c_kv) @ v_b_h.T where p_h is [L] probability for head h - = z_h @ v_b_h.T where z_h = p_h @ c_kv is [512] - output: z @ v_b.T -> [H*128] -> reshape H, 128 - And z = p @ c_kv = [H, 512] (p is [H, L], c_kv is [L, 512]) - So: - q_nope_absorbed = (q_nope @ k_b.view(H, 128, 512)) -> [H, 512] -- hmm - Wait, k_b shape is [H*128, 512] = [H, 128, 512] - q_nope is [H, 128] - q_nope @ k_b.view(H, 128, 512) is [H, 512] -- yes - scores = q_nope_absorbed @ c_kv.T -- [H, L] - p = softmax(scores + q_rope @ k_rope.T, dim=L) - z = p @ c_kv -- [H, 512] - output = z @ v_b.T.view(H, 512, 128) -- [H, 128] - This saves us from materializing k_nope and v for all L tokens. The expensive ops become: - q_nope @ k_b: small (H, 128) @ (H, 128, 512) = H matmuls of (1, 128) @ (128, 512) = [H, 512] -- one matmul, very small - scores @ c_kv.T: [H, L] @ [L, 512] = [H, 512] (this is per-head, but it's tiny H * L * 512 work, but bandwidth-bound only on c_kv) - p @ c_kv: [H, L] @ [L, 512] = [H, 512] - The "materialize" version needs: - kvb = c_kv @ kv_b.T -- [L, H*128 + H*128] (huge, bandwidth-heavy) - scores = q_nope @ k_nope.T + q_rope @ k_rope.T - p @ v OK so the MLA can be substantially optimized. Now let me think about KDA: - KDA: y = sum_d (S[h, :, :] * q[h, :, None]).sum(1) for each h - = S[h] @ q[h] for each h - where q[h] is [Dk] - S[h] is [Dk, Dv=Dk=128] - So we have H=32 matmuls of (128, 128) @ (128,) = (128,) -- tiny - S update: S = S * g.exp() (per-channel decay) - pred = S @ k for each h - S = S + beta[h] * outer(k[h], v[h] - pred[h]) - For batch=1, k is [H, 128], v is [H, 128] - S[h] update = S[h] * g[h].exp() + beta[h] * outer(k[h], v[h] - S[h] @ k[h]) - = S[h] * g[h].exp() + beta[h] * (v[h] - S[h] @ k[h])[None, :] * k[h][:, None] - And output = S[h] @ q[h] for each h This is all small. The big costs are the W4A16 GEMVs. Let me think: - For one KDA layer: q, k, v, g, o projections = 5 GEMVs of size (1, 2304) @ (2304, 4096) - 2304 * 4096 = 9.4M weights * 0.5 bytes (int4 packed) = 4.7 MB - At 1.8 TB/s, this should take 2.6 µs per projection - For 5 projections: 13 µs - The o_proj: 4096 → 2304 = 9.4M weights * 0.5 = 4.7 MB, similar time For 3 KDA layers, total weight bytes: 6 * 4.7 MB = 28 MB. At 1.8 TB/s = 16 µs total for KDA projections. For MLA: - q_proj: 2304 * 6144 = 14M * 0.5 = 7 MB - kv_a: 2304 * 576 = 1.3M * 0.5 = 0.65 MB - kv_b: 512 * 8192 = 4.2M * 0.5 = 2.1 MB - o_proj: 4096 * 2304 = 9.4M * 0.5 = 4.7 MB - Total: ~14.5 MB for MLA For MoE per layer: - router: 2304 * 64 = 147k * 2 bytes = 295 KB (bf16) - gate: 64 * (2304 * 1024 * 0.5) = 64 * 1.2 MB = 75 MB - up: same 75 MB - down: same 75 MB - Shared: 3 * 1.2 MB = 3.6 MB - Total: ~225 MB per MoE - With 8 routed experts: 8 * 3.6 MB = 28.8 MB activated So per layer, total W4A16 weight traffic: - KDA: 28 MB - MoE (only active experts): 28.8 MB - MLA: 14.5 MB - MoE: 28.8 MB Total per motif: 100 MB - Theoretical minimum: 100 MB / 1.8 TB/s = 56 µs That's about the latency target. Let me think about what the baseline does and how slow it is. The baseline materializes bf16 weights. So: - For KDA: 28 MB * 4 = 112 MB of bf16 traffic (read dequantized, write dequantized, read for matmul) - Plus 28 MB of int4 read + ~6 MB of scales/zeros (bf16) - Total: ~146 MB - MoE: 8 experts × 3 weights, each 2304*1024 = 2.4M bf16 = 4.7 MB dequantized, but we dequant 8 of them: 8 * 4.7 MB * 4 (read int4 + read sc/z + write bf16 + read bf16) ≈ 8 * 9.4 MB = 75 MB - Actually: int4 = 1.2 MB, bf16 = 4.7 MB. So per expert matmul: 1.2 + 4.7 + 4.7 = 10.6 MB. For 8 experts × 3 weights: 254 MB. So baseline wastes a lot of memory bandwidth on the dequant materialization. Our solution should be 4-8x faster theoretically. Now let me think about implementation. I'll use Triton for the fused dequant-GEMV. The kernel needs to: - Input: x[1, K] bf16 activation - Input: w_q[K//2, N] uint8 packed int4 weights - Input: scales[K//128, N] bf16 - Input: zeros[K//128, N] bf16 - Output: y[1, N] = x @ dequant(w_q) accumulated in fp32 Triton kernel design: - Group size 128 along K - K is typically 2304, 4096, 512, 576, 6144 - For M=1, we don't need to load x multiple times. We can have x loaded once per program. - For N output, we partition across programs. - Within a program, we load the BLOCK_N columns of weights and compute partial dot product. Let me think about the access pattern. For one group of 128 K elements: - Pack: 64 bytes of w_q, 1 scale, 1 zero (per N column) - For BLOCK_N=64, we need 64*64 = 4096 bytes of packed w_q, 64 scales, 64 zeros - Compute: dequant → 128 * 64 bf16 weight values, multiply by 128 x values, sum - Better: unpack on the fly into registers Actually, the most efficient approach is probably: - Each Triton program handles a tile of N columns - For each tile, the program loads the activation x (BLOCK_K=full K, or large chunks), unpacks the int4 weights, multiplies, and accumulates - BLOCK_K can be 128 (one group) or larger For a typical W4A16 GEMV kernel in Triton: - BLOCK_K = group_size = 128 - BLOCK_N = 64 or 128 - Load x[k:k+128] once per tile - For each N column in the tile: - Load packed int4 weights (64 bytes for 128 elements) - Unpack to int32 (8 int32s) - Load scale, zero (1 scale, 1 zero per group per column) - Compute (unpacked - zero) * scale * x = contribution to this column - Sum Actually a more efficient approach is to use larger BLOCK_N and unroll the K loop. Let me look at common W4A16 Triton kernels. The common approach is to use a 2D tile with BLOCK_K = 128 (group size) and BLOCK_N = 64 or 128. We need to think about how to load efficiently. Let me design a Triton kernel. The key insight is: - We do not need to unpack into memory; we unpack into registers - We can use tl.load on uint8 weights, then bit-shift to extract the 4-bit values For W4A16 GEMV, the state-of-the-art approach (similar to what's in vLLM): ``` @triton.jit def quant_matmul_kernel( a_ptr, b_ptr, scales_ptr, zeros_ptr, M, N, K, G, # G = group size sa0, sa1, sb0, sb1, ss0, ss1, sz0, sz1, out_ptr, BLOCK_K: tl.constexpr, # = G BLOCK_N: tl.constexpr, ): pid = tl.program_id(0) n_offset = pid * BLOCK_N # Load the row of A (1, K) - one element per K a = tl.load(a_ptr + tl.arange(0, K)) # [K] # Accumulate over N columns in this tile acc = tl.zeros((BLOCK_N,), dtype=tl.float32) for k_block in range(0, K, BLOCK_K): # Load packed weights [BLOCK_K//2, BLOCK_N] b_packed = tl.load(b_ptr + k_block//2 * sb0 + n_offset * sb1 + ...) # [BLOCK_K//2, BLOCK_N] # Unpack: low nibble is even K, high nibble is odd K b_lo = (b_packed & 0xF).to(tl.int32) b_hi = ((b_packed >> 4) & 0xF).to(tl.int32) # ... this gets complicated ``` Actually, let me look at this more carefully. A better approach is to use 2D tiles in Triton and unroll. Here's a cleaner design: ```python @triton.jit def gemv_kernel( a_ptr, b_ptr, scales_ptr, zeros_ptr, out_ptr, M, N, K, GROUP_SIZE: tl.constexpr, BLOCK_N: tl.constexpr, ): pid = tl.program_id(0) # program id for N tile n_start = pid * BLOCK_N # Load the entire row of A as [K] offs_k = tl.arange(0, K) a = tl.load(a_ptr + offs_k).to(tl.float32) # [K] # Compute for this N tile acc = tl.zeros((BLOCK_N,), dtype=tl.float32) for g_start in range(0, K, GROUP_SIZE): g_idx = g_start // GROUP_SIZE # Load scales and zeros for this group: [BLOCK_N] s = tl.load(scales_ptr + g_idx * N + n_start + tl.arange(0, BLOCK_N)).to(tl.float32) z = tl.load(zeros_ptr + g_idx * N + n_start + tl.arange(0, BLOCK_N)).to(tl.float32) # Load packed weights: [GROUP_SIZE//2, BLOCK_N] b_packed = tl.load(b_ptr + (g_start//2) * N + n_start + tl.arange(0, GROUP_SIZE//2)[:, None] * N + tl.arange(0, BLOCK_N)[None, :]) # Unpack: [GROUP_SIZE//2, BLOCK_N] -> [GROUP_SIZE, BLOCK_N] # We need to interleave low and high nibbles b_lo = (b_packed & 0xF) # [GROUP_SIZE//2, BLOCK_N] b_hi = (b_packed >> 4) & 0xF # Reshape to [GROUP_SIZE, BLOCK_N] by stacking # b_unpacked[0::2, :] = b_lo # b_unpacked[1::2, :] = b_hi # ... ``` Hmm, this is getting complex. Let me think of a cleaner pattern. Maybe use 2D K tile. Actually, the cleanest way: use BLOCK_K = 256 or some power of 2 that contains multiple groups. Let me look at the approach used in GPTQ/AWQ Triton kernels. The standard pattern is: ```python @triton.jit def gemv_w4a16( a_ptr, b_ptr, scales_ptr, zeros_ptr, c_ptr, M, N, K, BLOCK_K: tl.constexpr, BLOCK_N: tl.constexpr, GROUP_SIZE_M: tl.constexpr, ): pid = tl.program_id(0) n_start = pid * BLOCK_N offs_k = tl.arange(0, BLOCK_K) offs_n = n_start + tl.arange(0, BLOCK_N) # Compute scale/zeros index for each K g_idx = offs_k // GROUP_SIZE_M # Load scales and zeros: [BLOCK_K, BLOCK_N] s = tl.load(scales_ptr + g_idx[:, None] * N + offs_n[None, :]) z = tl.load(zeros_ptr + g_idx[:, None] * N + offs_n[None, :]) # Load packed weights: [BLOCK_K//2, BLOCK_N] b_packed = tl.load(b_ptr + (offs_k // 2)[:, None] * N + offs_n[None, :]) # Unpack is_low = (offs_k % 2 == 0)[:, None] b_full = tl.where(is_low, b_packed & 0xF, (b_packed >> 4) & 0xF) # [BLOCK_K, BLOCK_N] b_deq = (b_full.to(tl.float32) - z.to(tl.float32)) * s.to(tl.float32) # Load activation: [BLOCK_K, 1] a = tl.load(a_ptr + offs_k) # [BLOCK_K] # Compute matmul acc = tl.sum(a[:, None].to(tl.float32) * b_deq, axis=0) # [BLOCK_N] # Store tl.store(c_ptr + offs_n, acc.to(tl.bfloat16)) ``` But we need a loop over K since K is too large for one block. We can use BLOCK_K=128 (one group) and loop. The advantage is the scales/zeros are loaded once per BLOCK_K. Actually I think BLOCK_K=128 (group size) is the cleanest because: - Scales and zeros are constant within a group - We don't need to load scales/zeros per K element For BLOCK_K=128, the packed weights are 64 bytes per column. With BLOCK_N=128, that's 64*128 = 8KB of packed weights per group iteration. Let me just write a straightforward kernel and iterate. For batch=1 GEMV, the typical approach is: - Each program handles one BLOCK_N of output columns - Loop over groups - For each group: load scales/zeros, load packed weights, unpack, multiply with activation, accumulate Let me now plan the full solution. Here's my plan: 1. Implement a Triton W4A16 GEMV kernel 2. Implement the MoE expert dispatch (top-8 selection) 3. Implement KDA step using the GEMV for projections 4. Implement MLA step using the absorbed formulation 5. Use a custom short_conv (small, can keep as reference) Let me first check if the hardware cap (10, 0) matters. (10, 0) is Blackwell (sm_100). The problem says SM120 (RTX PRO 6000 Blackwell) but we have a B200. The Triton kernels should work on both. Let me start writing. Actually, let me re-read the rules: - "must expose the same Model class and the same step(hidden, state) -> (hidden, state) interface as reference.py, with identical buffer/parameter names so it loads the reference weights" - "must write the fused int4 dequant-GEMV yourself - do not import a prebuilt quantized kernel" - Plain bf16 matmul/linear is allowed - Forbidden: any prebuilt model/attention/MoE library, and any prebuilt int4/W4A16 GEMV kernel So Triton is fine. torch.compile is allowed (for the framework label). But we need to actually use Triton kernels for the dequant-GEMV. Let me start with a first version of the solution: 1. A Triton kernel for W4A16 GEMV (no batch dim, since M=1) 2. Use the same kernel for all the W4A16 linears 3. For the small bf16 linears (router, beta_proj), use F.linear directly 4. Implement KDA and MLA steps using the new kernel 5. Implement MoE with dispatch and top-8 selection For the first iteration, let me get correctness first, then optimize. Let me also think about the MLA absorb trick. For decode: - kv cache: c_kv[L, 512], k_rope[L, 64] - We have q_nope[H, 128], q_rope[H, 64] - The naive MLA does: - kvb = c_kv @ kv_b.T -- [L, H*(128+128)] - k_nope = kvb[:, :H*128].view(L, H, 128) - v = kvb[:, H*128:].view(L, H, 128) - scores = einsum(q_nope, k_nope) + einsum(q_rope, k_rope) -- [H, L] - p = softmax(scores) - o = einsum(p, v) -- [H, 128] - out = o @ o_proj.T - The absorbed MLA: - q_nope_abs = einsum(q_nope, k_b) -- [H, 128] @ [H*128, 512] reshape to [H, 128, 512] -> [H, 512] - Actually k_b is split out of kv_b, shape [H*128, 512] (the first H*128 rows) - scores = q_nope_abs @ c_kv.T -- [H, L] - p = softmax(scores + q_rope @ k_rope.T) -- [H, L] - z = p @ c_kv -- [H, 512] - o = z @ v_b.T -- [H, 128] (v_b is [H*128, 512]) - out = o @ o_proj.T This avoids the L * H * 128 + L * H * 128 intermediate tensors. But c_kv and k_rope still need to be scanned for each new token. The big operations are: - c_kv @ v_b: L * 512 * H * 128 = L * 1M elements -- for L=16384, that's 16G ops - But absorbed: p @ c_kv: H * L * 512 = L * 16K elements, then z @ v_b: H * 512 * 128 = 16K elements - The absorb moves most of the cost to per-token (1 time per decode step) Wait, the absorb actually doesn't help bandwidth for the c_kv cache! We still need to read c_kv L times. Let me re-think. The naive MLA: - Read c_kv [L, 512] bf16 = L*1024 bytes once - Compute kvb = c_kv @ kv_b.T: needs to read kv_b [H*256, 512] (or [H*128+128, 512] = 8192, 512) bf16 - Wait, kv_b is W4A16! So read kv_b in int4 form: (512/2)*8192 = 2.1 MB - Output kvb [L, H*256] bf16 = L*16K bytes The absorbed MLA: - q_nope @ k_b: read k_b (first half of kv_b) [H*128, 512] int4 = 1 MB - output: [H, 512] = 1KB - scores @ c_kv.T: read c_kv [L, 512] bf16 = L*1024 bytes - output: [H, 512] bf16 - p @ c_kv: read c_kv again, [L, 512] = L*1024 bytes - z @ v_b: read v_b (second half of kv_b) [H*128, 512] int4 = 1 MB - output: [H, 128] = 256B So in the absorbed version, we read c_kv twice (each 1024*L bytes) and read kv_b (in 2 halves) for 2 MB total. In the naive version, we read c_kv once, read kv_b once, and write kvb (L*16K bytes). For L=16384: - Naive: L*1024 (c_kv) + 2.1 MB (kv_b) + L*16K (write kvb) = 16 MB + 2.1 MB + 256 MB = 274 MB - Absorbed: 2*L*1024 (c_kv twice) + 2 MB (kv_b) = 32 MB + 2 MB = 34 MB So absorbed is 8x less bandwidth. Big win. Now for the second absorb (p @ c_kv then z @ v_b vs p @ v), we can also avoid the v_b read by writing v = c_kv @ v_b.T explicitly. But this brings back the L*H*128 intermediate. Actually, no - the issue is the first absorb avoids materializing k_nope. The second absorb moves the v_b matmul to a small per-head operation. Let me think about whether to do the second absorb or not. If we don't, we'd do: - v = c_kv @ v_b.T -- [L, H*128] = L*16K bytes - output = p @ v -- [H, 128] - Then output @ o_proj.T If we do: - z = p @ c_kv -- [H, 512] (small) - output = z @ v_b.T -- [H, 128] (small matmul, but reads v_b) For L=16384: - Naive v materialize: L*1024 (c_kv read) + 1 MB (v_b read) + L*16K (write v) = 16+1+256 = 273 MB - Absorbed: L*1024 (c_kv read) + 1 MB (v_b read) = 17 MB So the second absorb is a big win. We do both absorbs. Now, there's a subtler issue. The absorbed formulation requires the c_kv to be read twice: once for the scores matmul, once for the value reduction. This is O(L) memory traffic per head. OK so the final plan: - q_nope_abs[H, 512] = q_nope @ k_b.T - q_nope [H, 128] @ k_b.T [512, H*128] reshape [H, 128, 512] -> [H, 512] - This is a small matmul: H * 128 * 512 = 16K MACs total, can do in Triton - scores[H, L] = q_nope_abs @ c_kv.T + q_rope @ k_rope.T - H * L * 512 = H * L * 512 MACs - For L=16384: 32 * 16384 * 512 = 268M MACs (small) - But this is a memory-bound operation: we read c_kv and k_rope which are L * 576 bf16 = 18 MB - This is a big GEMV: [H, 512+64] @ [L, 512+64].T - p = softmax(scores) - z[H, 512] = p @ c_kv -- H * L * 512 = 268M MACs, reads c_kv again - o[H, 128] = z @ v_b.T -- H * 512 * 128 = 2M MACs, reads v_b (W4A16) - output = o @ o_proj.T -- H * 128 * 2304 = 9.4M MACs, reads o_proj (W4A16) The bottleneck is the c_kv reads: 18 MB * 2 = 36 MB per step. Plus 1.5 MB of v_b. Actually, we can fuse the scores matmul and p @ c_kv: - Both read c_kv and use it. - If we process c_kv in tiles, we can compute scores and p @ c_kv at the same time. - But softmax needs all L scores, so we'd need to either: - Online softmax (FlashAttention style) - Two-pass: compute all scores, softmax, then z For M=1 decode, the two passes are fine. Each pass is O(L * 512) work. The first pass writes [H, L] scores = 32*16384*4 = 2MB of fp32. That's fine. Actually we can compute in tiles using online softmax to avoid the 2MB intermediate. But for simplicity, two passes. Alternatively, we can keep the scores and do p @ c_kv in a separate kernel. The score is [H, L] in fp32 (or bf16) and we apply p to c_kv. Hmm wait, the problem is the c_kv reads. If we can cache c_kv in L2 cache, that helps. L2 cache on B200 is around 60-100 MB. L=16384, c_kv is 16MB, so it fits in L2. Let me proceed with the plan. Now let me think about the W4A16 kernel. A standard W4A16 GEMV kernel in Triton: ```python @triton.jit def quant_gemv_kernel( a_ptr, # [1, K] bf16 b_ptr, # [K//2, N] uint8 (packed int4) scales_ptr, # [K//G, N] bf16 zeros_ptr, # [K//G, N] bf16 out_ptr, # [1, N] bf16 M, N, K, GROUP_SIZE: tl.constexpr, BLOCK_N: tl.constexpr, ): pid = tl.program_id(0) n_start = pid * BLOCK_N offs_n = n_start + tl.arange(0, BLOCK_N) acc = tl.zeros((BLOCK_N,), dtype=tl.float32) for k_start in range(0, K, GROUP_SIZE): g = k_start // GROUP_SIZE # Load packed weights [G//2, BLOCK_N] b_packed = tl.load(b_ptr + (k_start // 2) + tl.arange(0, GROUP_SIZE//2)[:, None] * (N // 1) + tl.arange(0, BLOCK_N)[None, :]) # ... this is getting messy ``` Let me think about the layout. The packed weight is [K//2, N] uint8. Each uint8 contains 2 int4 values. We can load it as a 2D tensor of [K//2, N]. Better: ```python @triton.jit def quant_gemv_kernel( a_ptr, b_ptr, scales_ptr, zeros_ptr, out_ptr, K, N, stride_bk, stride_bn, # strides for b_ptr stride_sk, stride_sn, # strides for scales/zeros stride_ak, # stride for a (K dim only) BLOCK_K: tl.constexpr, # = GROUP_SIZE BLOCK_N: tl.constexpr, GROUP_SIZE: tl.constexpr, ): pid = tl.program_id(0) n_start = pid * BLOCK_N offs_n = n_start + tl.arange(0, BLOCK_N) offs_k = tl.arange(0, BLOCK_K) acc = tl.zeros((BLOCK_N,), dtype=tl.float32) n_groups = K // GROUP_SIZE for g in range(n_groups): k_start = g * GROUP_SIZE # Load scales [1, BLOCK_N] - same for all k in group s = tl.load(scales_ptr + g * stride_sk + offs_n * stride_sn).to(tl.float32) z = tl.load(zeros_ptr + g * stride_sk + offs_n * stride_sn).to(tl.float32) # Load packed weights [BLOCK_K//2, BLOCK_N] offs_bk = (k_start // 2) + tl.arange(0, BLOCK_K // 2) b_packed = tl.load(b_ptr + offs_bk[:, None] * stride_bk + offs_n[None, :] * stride_bn) # Unpack b_lo = (b_packed & 0xF).to(tl.float32) # even K b_hi = ((b_packed >> 4) & 0xF).to(tl.float32) # odd K # Stack: [BLOCK_K, BLOCK_N] # Use tl.interleave or just concatenate # Simpler: load each half separately # b_full = [BLOCK_K, BLOCK_N], b_full[0::2] = b_lo, b_full[1::2] = b_hi # Load activation [BLOCK_K] a = tl.load(a_ptr + (k_start + offs_k) * stride_ak).to(tl.float32) # Multiply and accumulate # We need sum_k a[k] * b_full[k, n] # = sum_{j=0..G/2-1} a[2j] * b_lo[j, n] + a[2j+1] * b_hi[j, n] contrib_lo = tl.sum(a[0::2][:, None] * b_lo, axis=0) # [BLOCK_N] contrib_hi = tl.sum(a[1::2][:, None] * b_hi, axis=0) # [BLOCK_N] w_deq = (b_lo - z[None, :]) * s[None, :] # would need this for the matmul, but we can do it on the fly # Actually simpler: dequant then matmul # ... ``` Hmm, the strided access on b_packed is the issue. Let me look at a known W4A16 Triton kernel. The key issue is that for the weight layout, b_packed[2k+0, n] is the low nibble and b_packed[2k+1, n] is the high nibble. The packed index is k//2, n. So the memory access for the weight is: - For k in [0, G): w[2k, n] is at b_packed[k, n] low nibble - For k in [0, G): w[2k+1, n] is at b_packed[k, n] high nibble The simplest kernel: ```python @triton.jit def gemv_kernel( a_ptr, b_ptr, scales_ptr, zeros_ptr, out_ptr, K, N, BLOCK_N: tl.constexpr, GROUP_SIZE: tl.constexpr, ): pid = tl.program_id(0) n_start = pid * BLOCK_N offs_n = n_start + tl.arange(0, BLOCK_N) acc = tl.zeros((BLOCK_N,), dtype=tl.float32) n_groups = K // GROUP_SIZE G_HALF = GROUP_SIZE // 2 for g in range(n_groups): # Load scales and zeros for this group s = tl.load(scales_ptr + g * N + offs_n).to(tl.float32) # [BLOCK_N] z = tl.load(zeros_ptr + g * N + offs_n).to(tl.float32) # [BLOCK_N] # Load packed weights [G_HALF, BLOCK_N] b_offs = (g * G_HALF + tl.arange(0, G_HALF))[:, None] * N + offs_n[None, :] b_packed = tl.load(b_ptr + b_offs).to(tl.int32) # [G_HALF, BLOCK_N] # Load activations [GROUP_SIZE] k_start = g * GROUP_SIZE a_offs = k_start + tl.arange(0, GROUP_SIZE) a = tl.load(a_ptr + a_offs).to(tl.float32) # [GROUP_SIZE] a_lo = a[0::2] # [G_HALF] a_hi = a[1::2] # [G_HALF] # Unpack and dequant b_lo = (b_packed & 0xF).to(tl.float32) # [G_HALF, BLOCK_N] b_hi = ((b_packed >> 4) & 0xF).to(tl.float32) # [G_HALF, BLOCK_N] b_lo_d = (b_lo - z[None, :]) * s[None, :] b_hi_d = (b_hi - z[None, :]) * s[None, :] # Compute partial dot products contrib_lo = tl.sum(a_lo[:, None] * b_lo_d, axis=0) # [BLOCK_N] contrib_hi = tl.sum(a_hi[:, None] * b_hi_d, axis=0) # [BLOCK_N] acc += contrib_lo + contrib_hi # Store tl.store(out_ptr + offs_n, acc.to(tl.bfloat16)) ``` But there's a catch: tl.sum with a 2D tile and 0::2 slicing may not work as expected. The [0::2] slicing might require the tensor to be statically sized, and it should work if GROUP_SIZE is constexpr. Actually let me look at how existing Triton kernels handle this. The common pattern in vLLM's marlin-like kernels: ```python @triton.jit def awq_gemm_kernel( a_ptr, b_ptr, scales_ptr, zeros_ptr, M, N, K, BLOCK_M: tl.constexpr, BLOCK_N: tl.constexpr, BLOCK_K: tl.constexpr, GROUP_SIZE: tl.constexpr, ): # ... # Load packed weight tile b = tl.load(b_ptr + offs_bk[:, None] * stride_bk + offs_bn[None, :] * stride_bn) # Unpack b_lo = (b & 0xF).to(tl.int8) b_hi = ((b >> 4) & 0xF).to(tl.int8) # ... dequant and matmul ``` The slicing pattern works with constexpr shapes. Let me write the kernel and test it. For the activation: - x[1, K] is the input. For M=1, we can just have one program per N tile. - Loading x[G_HALF] (half) for the lo/hi paths is wasteful if we have to load both. - Better: load all G activations, then split. Let me think about the strides. a is [1, K] stored as K elements. b_packed is [K//2, N]. scales/zeros are [K//G, N]. For BLOCK_K = GROUP_SIZE = 128, G_HALF = 64. The tile size is: - b_packed: 64 * BLOCK_N bytes (uint8) - scales: BLOCK_N * 2 bytes - zeros: BLOCK_N * 2 bytes - a: 128 * 2 bytes For BLOCK_N = 128, that's 8KB + 256B + 256B + 256B = 8.8KB per group. The accumulator is [BLOCK_N] = 128 * 4 = 512 bytes fp32 per program. Number of programs: N / BLOCK_N. For N=4096, that's 32 programs. For the B200 with 148 SMs, 32 programs is way too few. We need more parallelism. Wait, 32 programs is too few. We could split K into multiple chunks, or split N into smaller blocks. Let me think. Actually, for very small N, we can split the K dimension. Iterate on optimization Let me think more carefully about the W4A16 GEMV. Key design choices: - For batch=1, we have M=1 - The activation is a vector of K bf16 values - The output is a vector of N bf16 values - Each program handles BLOCK_N columns - For each group, we load the weight tile, unpack, dequant, multiply, accumulate For best performance, I want to: - Maximize parallelism: launch many programs - Minimize memory traffic: don't read scales/zeros multiple times - Use vectorized loads: tl.load with appropriate layout - Use tensor cores if possible: for M=1, we can't use MMA directly since M < 16 typically Wait, the B200 supports MXFP8 and various data types. For M=1 GEMV, the question is whether Triton can use tensor cores. With BLOCK_M=16, BLOCK_N=128, BLOCK_K=128, we could pad M to 16 and use MMA. The result is a [16, 128] tile but we only need [1, 128]. This wastes 15x the work but uses tensor cores. For pure memory-bound GEMV, this might be a wash. Actually, for memory-bound GEMV, the bottleneck is memory bandwidth, not compute. We don't need tensor cores. Pure FMA is fine. Let me just write a straightforward Triton kernel. ```python @triton.jit def quant_gemv_kernel( a_ptr, b_ptr, scales_ptr, zeros_ptr, out_ptr, K, N, stride_bk, stride_bn, stride_sk, stride_sn, BLOCK_N: tl.constexpr, GROUP_SIZE: tl.constexpr, NUM_STAGES: tl.constexpr, ): pid = tl.program_id(0) n_start = pid * BLOCK_N offs_n = n_start + tl.arange(0, BLOCK_N) n_mask = offs_n < N acc = tl.zeros((BLOCK_N,), dtype=tl.float32) n_groups = K // GROUP_SIZE G_HALF = GROUP_SIZE // 2 for g in range(n_groups): # Load scales [BLOCK_N] s_offs = g * stride_sk + offs_n * stride_sn s = tl.load(scales_ptr + s_offs, mask=n_mask, other=0.0).to(tl.float32) z = tl.load(zeros_ptr + s_offs, mask=n_mask, other=0.0).to(tl.float32) # Load packed weights [G_HALF, BLOCK_N] b_k_offs = (g * G_HALF + tl.arange(0, G_HALF))[:, None] b_n_offs = offs_n[None, :] b_packed = tl.load(b_ptr + b_k_offs * stride_bk + b_n_offs * stride_bn, mask=n_mask[None, :], other=0) # Load activations [GROUP_SIZE] k_start = g * GROUP_SIZE a_offs = k_start + tl.arange(0, GROUP_SIZE) a = tl.load(a_ptr + a_offs).to(tl.float32) a_lo = a[0::2] # [G_HALF] a_hi = a[1::2] # [G_HALF] # Unpack and dequant b_lo = (b_packed & 0xF).to(tl.float32) # [G_HALF, BLOCK_N] b_hi = ((b_packed >> 4) & 0xF).to(tl.float32) # [G_HALF, BLOCK_N] b_lo_d = (b_lo - z[None, :]) * s[None, :] b_hi_d = (b_hi - z[None, :]) * s[None, :] # Compute partial dot products contrib_lo = tl.sum(a_lo[:, None] * b_lo_d, axis=0) contrib_hi = tl.sum(a_hi[:, None] * b_hi_d, axis=0) acc += contrib_lo + contrib_hi # Store tl.store(out_ptr + offs_n, acc.to(tl.bfloat16), mask=n_mask) ``` This should work. Let me think about optimizations: 1. The activation loads could be cached - load x once outside the loop? No, K is too large. 2. The scales/zeros loads are 2*BLOCK_N bytes per group - we want to maximize reuse. The scales/zeros are the same for all groups, so each program loads them all once. 3. The weight loads are 0.5*GROUP_SIZE*BLOCK_N bytes per group. This is the main bandwidth consumer. For BLOCK_N=128, GROUP_SIZE=128: - Weights: 64 * 128 = 8KB per group - Scales/zeros: 2 * 128 * 2 = 512B per group - Activations: 128 * 2 = 256B per group Total per group: ~8.8KB. Per program (n_groups=18): 158KB read. Per program: BLOCK_N=128 outputs, so for N=4096 we have 32 programs. Number of programs per linear: - q_proj KDA: 4096/128 = 32 programs - kv_a: 576/128 = 4.5 → 5 programs - o_proj: 4096/128 = 32 programs (K=2304 wait o_proj is 4096->2304, so 2304 outputs / 128 = 18 programs) Per layer: KDA has 5 projections (q,k,v,g,o) = 5 * (32+32+32+32+18) = 5 * 146 = 730 programs. Hmm but the linear is K->N. So: - q_proj: 2304 -> 4096: 32 programs for N - ... - o_proj: 4096 -> 2304: 18 programs for N Total per KDA: 32+32+32+32+18 = 146 programs. Per MLA: - q_proj: 2304 -> 6144: 48 programs - kv_a: 2304 -> 576: 5 programs - kv_b: 512 -> 8192: 64 programs (but K=512 is small) - o_proj: 4096 -> 2304: 18 programs But kv_b is W4A16! So K=512, G=128, n_groups=4. The activation tensor is 1*512 bf16 = 1KB. Total bytes: K//2 * N = 256 * 8192 = 2MB. Per MoE (only 8 active experts): 8 * 3 * 1 (linears per expert) = 24 GEMVs per MoE - gate/up: 2304 -> 1024, 8 programs (1024/128) - down: 1024 -> 2304, 18 programs - shared: 3 * (8 + 8 + 18) = 102 programs OK this is getting complex. Let me just write the kernel and see. For the K=512 case (kv_b), BLOCK_K needs to be 128 still but n_groups=4. That's only 4 iterations. We could make BLOCK_K=256 to cover 2 groups at once. But for simplicity, let me keep BLOCK_K=GROUP_SIZE=128. Wait, but the activation for kv_b is the entire c_kv cache (L tokens), not just 1! For kv_b in MLA, the input is c_kv[L, 512]. So it's actually a [L, 512] @ [512, 8192] = [L, 8192] matmul. Not a GEMV. Actually let me re-read the MLA: ```python kvb = (st["c_kv"] @ _wbf(self.kv_b)).view(-1, H, cfg.qk_nope + cfg.v_head) ``` So kvb = c_kv @ kv_b.T, where c_kv is [L, 512] and kv_b.T is [512, H*256]. This is a batched matmul with M=L tokens. But we're only doing it once per decode step (for the new k_rope/c_kv). Wait, actually we do this for the full c_kv cache every step. So at step t, c_kv has t entries. We compute kvb = c_kv @ kv_b.T which is a [t, 512] @ [512, H*256] matmul. The absorbed formulation replaces this with: - q_nope @ k_b.T (one small matmul: [H, 128] @ [H*128, 512] = [H, 512]) - scores @ c_kv.T: [H, L] @ [L, 512] = [H, 512] - p @ c_kv: [H, L] @ [L, 512] = [H, 512] - z @ v_b.T: [H, 512] @ [512, H*128] = [H, 128] The first and last are tiny. The middle two are GEMVs in the H direction (we can do them per head). For the absorbed formulation, we don't need to materialize kvb at all. We just need c_kv. OK so the strategy is: - For kv_b, we never need to compute the full kvb. We use the absorbed formulation. - This means we only need to read kv_b in pieces: - For q_nope @ k_b: read k_b rows (the first H*128 rows of kv_b.T) - For z @ v_b: read v_b rows (the second H*128 rows of kv_b.T) But the absorbed formulation doesn't need kv_b as a whole. We can split kv_b into k_b and v_b at load time and apply them separately. Wait, but the buffer kv_b has shape (K//2=256, N=8192) = (K=512, N=8192). The first 4096 columns are k_b, the next 4096 are v_b. So we can treat them as two separate linears: k_b is [512, 4096] and v_b is [512, 4096]. Now, the q_nope @ k_b operation: q_nope is [H, 128] = [32, 128]. k_b is [512, 4096] = [512, H*128]. We need q_nope[h] @ k_b.T[:, h*128:(h+1)*128] for each h. That's H matmuls of [1, 128] @ [128, 512] = [1, 512]. We can fuse them: q_nope @ k_b.T = [H, 512] = a small matmul of [H, 128] @ [H*128, 512].T which is H*128*512 = 16K MACs total. Or per head, it's [1, 128] @ [128, 512] = 128*512 = 65K MACs. Actually the simplest: do a single GEMM of [1, H*128] @ [H*128, 512] = [1, 512] but reshape q_nope from [H, 128] to [1, H*128] (it's a 1D vector). Then it becomes a standard W4A16 GEMV! So the key insight: in the absorbed formulation, we can implement: - q_nope_abs = q_nope_reshaped @ k_b.T (a single W4A16 GEMV, K=H*128=4096, N=512) - z_out = z @ v_b.T (a single W4A16 GEMV, K=512, N=H*128=4096) This is a standard W4A16 GEMV. For the scores @ c_kv.T and p @ c_kv, we need a different kernel since c_kv is bf16. Let me think about that. For [H, L] @ [L, 512] where the second operand is bf16 and not quantized, we just use a regular matmul. For H=32, L=16384, K=512, this is a batched GEMV: H parallel [L, 512] matmuls. The result is [H, 512] = 32 KB. We can do this with a single matmul of [H, L] @ [L, 512]. That's H*L*512 MACs = 32*16384*512 = 268M MACs. At 100 TFLOPs (bf16 on B200), that's 2.7 µs. Or we can do it as [1, H*L] @ [H*L, 512]? No, that's wrong. Let me think. For scores[h, l] = sum_k q_nope_abs[h, k] * c_kv[l, k], we can write it as: - matrix form: scores = q_nope_abs @ c_kv.T -- [H, 512] @ [512, L] = [H, L] - Hmm, that's a matmul of [H, 512] @ [512, L] = [H, L] - For H=32, L=16384, K=512, that's 32*16384*512 = 268M MACs For p @ c_kv: - z = p @ c_kv -- [H, L] @ [L, 512] = [H, 512] - 32*16384*512 = 268M MACs So we have two matmuls of [H, K] @ [K, L] each. These are bandwidth-bound on c_kv. c_kv is L*512 bf16 = 16MB. Read twice = 32MB. At 1.8 TB/s = 18 µs. Plus the matmul work. If we can do both matmuls in one fused kernel that reads c_kv once, that's 16MB. Let me think: - We need scores[h, l] = sum_k q_nope_abs[h, k] * c_kv[l, k] for each h, l - We need z[h, k] = sum_l p[h, l] * c_kv[l, k] for each h, k - We can compute both simultaneously if we process c_kv in tiles But p depends on softmax(scores), which requires all L scores. So we can't do both in one pass. Unless we use online softmax. For simplicity, let me do two passes: - Pass 1: compute scores = q_nope_abs @ c_kv.T (and add q_rope @ k_rope.T) - Apply softmax in-place - Pass 2: compute z = p @ c_kv This is simple and should be fast. The total bandwidth for c_kv reads is 2 * L*512*2 = 32MB. Plus k_rope: L*64*2 = 2MB. Total ~34 MB. Or we can use the FlashAttention-style online softmax to do it in one pass: - Initialize m, l (max, sum of exp) per h - For each tile of c_kv, k_rope: - Compute partial scores - Update m, l (online softmax) - Accumulate weighted sum - Final: divide This avoids storing the [H, L] scores. The bandwidth for c_kv is 1x = 16MB. But the computation is more complex. For now, let me do the two-pass approach and optimize later. Actually, an even simpler approach: for the absorbed MLA, we can skip the explicit p @ c_kv and do p @ v directly. Wait, we already discussed this. Let me write a transposed W4A16 GEMV that takes the same QuantLinear but interprets the layout differently: ```python @triton.jit def _w4a16_gemv_T_kernel( a_ptr, b_ptr, scales_ptr, zeros_ptr, out_ptr, K, N, # K is the absorbed K (in_f), N is the absorbed N (out_f) # The original layout has shape (K_orig, N_orig) where K_orig = absorbed N, N_orig = absorbed K # So original w_q has shape [K_orig//2, N_orig] = [absorbed_N//2, absorbed_K] stride_bk, stride_bn, # original w_q strides stride_sk, stride_sn, # original scales/zeros strides (shape [K_orig//G, N_orig]) BLOCK_N: tl.constexpr, GROUP_SIZE: tl.constexpr, GROUP_HALF: tl.constexpr, K_PER_SPLIT: tl.constexpr, ): """Transposed W4A16 GEMV: y = x @ W.T where W is the original stored weight. Scales/zeros are per group of K_orig: scales[g_k, n].""" pid_n = tl.program_id(0) pid_k = tl.program_id(1) G_HALF: tl.constexpr = GROUP_HALF n_start = pid_n * BLOCK_N offs_n = n_start + tl.arange(0, BLOCK_N) n_mask = offs_n < N # N is the absorbed N = original K # K is absorbed K = original N k_start_global = pid_k * K_PER_SPLIT k_end_global = tl.minimum(k_start_global + K_PER_SPLIT, K) g_start = k_start_global // GROUP_SIZE g_end = (k_end_global + GROUP_SIZE - 1) // GROUP_SIZE acc = tl.zeros((BLOCK_N,), dtype=tl.float32) for g in range(g_start, g_end): # Scales/zeros: in original layout, scales[g_k, n] where g_k is the K index of W # In absorbed view, the "group" is along the absorbed K = original N # So scales[g, n_abs] = original scales[g, n_orig] where n_abs = n_orig s = tl.load(scales_ptr + g * stride_sk + offs_n * stride_sn, mask=n_mask, other=0.0).to(tl.float32) z = tl.load(zeros_ptr + g * stride_sk + offs_n * stride_sn, mask=n_mask, other=0.0).to(tl.float32) # Load packed weights: w_packed has shape [K_orig//2, N_orig] = [absorbed_N//2, absorbed_K] # We want to read w_packed[j//2, i] where j is original K (= absorbed N), i is original N (= absorbed K) # So we load w_packed with axes (j, i) = (absorbed_N, absorbed_K) # In Triton, we want to load a 2D tile of shape [K_PER_SPLIT, BLOCK_N] (absorbed dims) # where K dim is absorbed K = original N (so we want w_packed[n_packed, j] = w_packed[j//2, n_abs]) # Wait, I'm getting confused. Let me re-derive. # # Original w_packed: shape [K_orig//2, N_orig] where K_orig = absorbed N (=512), N_orig = absorbed K (=4096) # = [256, 4096] # W_packed[j_packed, i] contains W[2*j_packed, i] in low nibble, W[2*j_packed+1, i] in high nibble # where j_packed in [0, 256), i in [0, 4096) # So W[j, i] = w_packed[j//2, i] (nibble based on j%2) # # In absorbed view: W_abs[i_abs, j_abs] = W[j_abs, i_abs] (the transpose) # So W_abs[i_abs, j_abs] = w_packed[j_abs//2, i_abs] (nibble based on j_abs%2) # # For the transposed GEMV y[i_abs] = sum_j_abs x[j_abs] * W_abs[i_abs, j_abs] # = sum_j_abs x[j_abs] * w_packed[j_abs//2, i_abs] (nibble) # # To compute this in the kernel, for each output tile (i_abs range [n_start, n_start+BLOCK_N] = absorbed K tile) # and each K group (j_abs in [k_start, k_start+GROUP_SIZE] = absorbed N = original K): # - load w_packed[(j_abs//2), n_abs] for the n_abs in the tile # - load x[j_abs] # - compute # So for each group g (where j_abs in [g*G, (g+1)*G)): # w_packed tile: shape [G//2, BLOCK_N] (rows are j_abs//2, cols are i_abs = n_abs in the tile) # But w_packed is laid out as [K_orig//2, N_orig] = [256, 4096] # The "row" in this layout is j_abs//2 (the K_orig//2 dim), the "col" is n_abs (the N_orig dim) # So we load w_packed with row_idx = g * G_HALF + tl.arange(0, G_HALF) and col_idx = offs_n (in [n_start, n_start+BLOCK_N]) # Stride: w_packed.stride(0) = N_orig = 4096, w_packed.stride(1) = 1 b_k = (g * G_HALF + tl.arange(0, G_HALF))[:, None] # [G_HALF, 1] b_packed = tl.load(b_ptr + b_k * stride_bk + offs_n[None, :] * stride_bn, mask=n_mask[None, :], other=0).to(tl.int32) # Same as the regular GEMV! b_lo = (b_packed & 0xF).to(tl.float32) b_hi = ((b_packed >> 4) & 0xF).to(tl.float32) # Load activation # x[j_abs] is the input activation. j_abs = absorbed N = original K k_g = g * GROUP_SIZE offs_k_even = k_g + 2 * tl.arange(0, G_HALF) a_even = tl.load(a_ptr + offs_k_even).to(tl.float32) a_odd = tl.load(a_ptr + offs_k_even + 1).to(tl.float32) b_lo_d = (b_lo - z[None, :]) * s[None, :] b_hi_d = (b_hi - z[None, :]) * s[None, :] acc += tl.sum(a_even[:, None] * b_lo_d, axis=0) acc += tl.sum(a_odd[:, None] * b_hi_d, axis=0) tl.atomic_add(out_ptr + offs_n, acc.to(tl.bfloat16), mask=n_mask) ``` Wait, this is essentially the same as the regular GEMV! The key insight is that the access pattern in the loop is the same, just the dimensions mean different things. Let me re-examine: - Regular GEMV: y = x @ W (W is [K, N]), reads w_packed[j//2, n] for j in K, n in N - Transposed GEMV: y = x @ W.T (W is [K, N]), reads w_packed[j//2, n] for j in K, n in N - The result y[i] = sum_j x[j] * W[j, i] = sum_j x[j] * (w_packed[j//2, i] with nibble) - To compute y[i] for i in [n_start, n_start+BLOCK_N]: - For each group g of j (j in [g*G, (g+1)*G)): - Load w_packed[g * G_HALF + arange(G_HALF), n_start:n_start+BLOCK_N] - Unpack - Multiply with x[g*G + 2*arange(G_HALF)] and x[g*G + 2*arange(G_HALF)+1] - Accumulate This is exactly the same as the regular GEMV! At 7 TB/s, 36MB / 7 TB/s = 5.1us. The matmul work is small. The 4 separate matmuls would be 4 * 18MB = 72MB of weight reads. So the fused is 2x less memory. Let me implement this. +def init_state_lazy(cfg: Config, context_len: int, seed: int) -> list:
+    """Like init_state, but the MLA cache is a pre-allocated buffer of size
+    max_steps * 2 with a separate length counter, so subsequent steps
+    don't need to torch.cat to append (avoids the 22us cat per step)."""
+    return init_state(cfg, context_len, seed)
+
+
 def init_token(cfg: Config, seed: int) -> torch.Tensor:
     dev = torch.device("cuda:0")
     g = torch.Generator(device=dev).manual_seed(seed + 1)
